  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_cfb60d56-fac8-493e-b6bb-8f61d928784c'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4236061941505601754">Ray Bradbury's second short story collection is back in print, its chilling encounters with funhouse mirrors, parasitic accident-watchers, and strange poker chips intact. Both sides of Bradbury's vaunted childhood nostalgia are also on display, in the celebratory "Uncle Einar," and haunting "The Lake," the latter a fine elegy to childhood loss. This edition features a new </span>
  <span id="freeText4236061941505601754" style="display:none">Ray Bradbury's second short story collection is back in print, its chilling encounters with funhouse mirrors, parasitic accident-watchers, and strange poker chips intact. Both sides of Bradbury's vaunted childhood nostalgia are also on display, in the celebratory "Uncle Einar," and haunting "The Lake," the latter a fine elegy to childhood loss. This edition features a new introduction by Bradbury, an invaluable essay on writing, wherein the author tells of his "Theater of Morning Voices," and, by inference, encourages you to listen to the same murmurings in yourself. And has any writer anywhere ever made such good use of exclamation marks!? (Illustrated by Joe Mugnaini.)<br /><br />Contents:<br /><br />· The Dwarf · ss Fantastic Jan/Feb ’54 <br />· The Next in Line · nv Dark Carnival, Arkham House: Sauk City, WI, 1947 <br />· The Watchful Poker Chip of H. Matisse · ss Beyond Fantasy Fiction Mar ’54 <br />· Skeleton · ss Weird Tales Sep ’45 <br />· The Jar · ss Weird Tales Nov ’44 <br />· The Lake · ss Weird Tales May ’44 <br />· The Emissary · ss Dark Carnival, Arkham House: Sauk City, WI, 1947 <br />· Touched with Fire [“Shopping for Death”] · ss Maclean’s Jun 1 ’54 <br />· The Small Assassin · ss Dime Mystery Magazine Nov ’46 <br />· The Crowd · ss Weird Tales May ’43 <br />· Jack-in-the-Box · ss Dark Carnival, Arkham House: Sauk City, WI, 1947 <br />· The Scythe · ss Weird Tales Jul ’43 <br />· Uncle Einar · ss Dark Carnival, Arkham House: Sauk City, WI, 1947 <br />· The Wind · ss Weird Tales Mar ’43 <br />· The Man Upstairs · ss Harper’s Mar ’47 <br />· There Was an Old Woman · ss Weird Tales Jul ’44 <br />· The Cistern · ss Mademoiselle May ’47 <br />· Homecoming · ss Mademoiselle Oct ’46 <br />· The Wonderful Death of Dudley Stone · ss Charm Jul ’54</span>
  <a data-text-id="4236061941505601754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<a href="/questions/422210-at-the-end-of-the-first-short-story-the"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[At the end of the first short story (The Dwarf) does the Dwarf take the pistol to shoot himself or does it run off with it to plan killing Ralph later? Does Ralph see himself in the mirror or the dwarf? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/22208207-tessa"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1373846554i/22208207._UY100_CR8,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/22208207-tessa">Tessa</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    I wasn't sure either, but my guess was that the dwarf runs off with the gun planning on shooting himself--hence why the girl runs after him, to stop h<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    I wasn't sure either, but my guess was that the dwarf runs off with the gun planning on shooting himself--hence why the girl runs after him, to stop him. If he was going to kill Ralph, why would he run away in the opposite direction with the gun? <br /><br />Ralph sees himself in the mirror, and the girl sees him too, and in the mirror's reflection Ralph appears shrunken and warped, which accurately reflects his inner self. When the dwarf looks in the mirror in the beginning of the story, he also saw his true self reflected--tall and handsome, which reflects his hidden talent and intelligence. That's why he was so drawn to the mirror room, because he could see himself as he wished the world would see him. <br /><br />So to me the entire story was, as cheesy as this sounds, about how the type of person someone is matters so much more than their outward appearance. And when the girl runs off to chase the dwarf, it was because she finally saw the type of person Ralph was inside and stopped indulging him--like saying that you shouldn't let someone's outward appearance fool you and should chase what really matters instead.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

The crisp air and long nights. The crisp air and long nights. Its perfect to read any time of year but its especially good during halloween time.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_423976090" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423976090" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/423976090?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345407856" href="/book/show/1091582.The_October_Country">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423976090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5005479150959565028">I love the reading of Bradbury on a crisp, autumn morning … sounds like October.<br/><br/>The October Country is a collection of short stories by the Grandmaster writer Ray Bradbury. <br/><br/>Eschewing any connection to science fiction, this group of purely fantasy tales resounds with Bradbury’s fascination with and brilliant creativity in the realms of the occult, macabre and the dark. <br/><br/>Bradbury begins the book with this explanation: “The October Country … that country where it is always turning late in the year. </span>
  <span id="freeText5005479150959565028" style="display:none">I love the reading of Bradbury on a crisp, autumn morning … sounds like October.<br><br>The October Country is a collection of short stories by the Grandmaster writer Ray Bradbury. <br><br>Eschewing any connection to science fiction, this group of purely fantasy tales resounds with Bradbury’s fascination with and brilliant creativity in the realms of the occult, macabre and the dark. <br><br>Bradbury begins the book with this explanation: “The October Country … that country where it is always turning late in the year. That country where the hills are fog and the rivers are mist; where noons go quickly, dusks and twilight linger, and midnights stay. That country composed in the main of cellars, sub-cellars, coal bins, closets, attics and pantries faced away from the sun. That country whose people are autumn people, thinking only autumn thoughts. Whose people passing at night on the empty walks sound like rain.” <br><br>	These are some of his best stories, told with imagination and his deft, descriptive virtuoso use of the language. His is a poetic prose, matched well with the theme of October. M. Night Shyamalan must have been influenced by Bradbury’s “Jack in the Box”, and his influence on modern fiction can be seen throughout the pages. From the odd “The Dwarf” to the creepy “The Jar” to the psychologically unsettling “The Cistern” Bradbury has woven a spider’s web of fiction made for cool days under a cloudy sky, with the autumn smells of campfires and frost. <br><br>Brew a witch’s mug of warm drink, breathe in the leafy chill, wrap up in an old woolen scarf and enjoy.<br><br>*** 2020 reread<br><br>I’m a Ray Bradbury fan and this is one of my favorite collections from him. This was first compiled in 1955 from previously released stories and a couple of these stories would be used again in his 2001 novel From the Dust Returned. Many of these have inspired other stories and episodes of the Twilight Zone and other shows.<br><br>Nineteen macabre and Octoberesque tales for our delight. They’re all good but for me the most notable are:<br><br>“Skeleton” – weird story where a man’s own skeleton seems to have a separate consciousness. Only a writer like RB could pull this off.<br><br>“The Jar” – Creepy and psychologically unsettling and then ends with a touch of outright horror.<br><br>“The Small Assassin” – Ray takes post-partum depression to another level.<br><br>“The Crowd” – a motor vehicle accident survivor notices some strange things about a crowd that surrounds him and goes on a quest for discovery that does not end well.<br><br>“Jack in the Box” – a story reminiscent of Shirly Jackson, a little boy being raised alone by his weird and psychopathic mother.<br><br>“Uncle Einar” and “Homecoming” are the two stories later used to put together From the Dust Returned, and describes an Addams Family like clan.<br><br>This is a must read for Bradbury fans and one any visitor to the October Country will enjoy. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1601558956i/30179023.jpg" width="204" height="320" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="5005479150959565028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2152766511" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2152766511" />
    <a title="Johann (jobis89)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50893023-johann-jobis89"><img alt="Johann (jobis89)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588540681p2/50893023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2152766511?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johann (jobis89)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johann (jobis89)" href="/user/show/50893023-johann-jobis89">Johann (jobis89)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345407856" href="/book/show/1091582.The_October_Country">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50893023-johann-jobis89?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2152766511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7860627543343440006">"...that country where it is always turning late in the year. That country where the hills are fog and the rivers are mist; where noons go quickly, dusks and twilights linger, and midnights stay. That country composed in the main of cellars, sub-cellars, coal-bins, closets, attics, and pantries faced away from the sun. That country whose people are autumn people, thinking only autumn thoughts. Whose people passing at night on the empty walks sound like rain..."<br/><br/>A collection of 19 macabre tales fr</span>
  <span id="freeText7860627543343440006" style="display:none">"...that country where it is always turning late in the year. That country where the hills are fog and the rivers are mist; where noons go quickly, dusks and twilights linger, and midnights stay. That country composed in the main of cellars, sub-cellars, coal-bins, closets, attics, and pantries faced away from the sun. That country whose people are autumn people, thinking only autumn thoughts. Whose people passing at night on the empty walks sound like rain..."<br /><br />A collection of 19 macabre tales from who many consider to be the finest writer of fantastic fiction.<br /><br />It is a truth universally acknowledged that the majority of short story collections are hit and miss. Well... this one is all killer and no filler, baby!! (Correction: perhaps a teeny tiny bit of filler as there was ONE story I just didn't vibe with - namely The Watchful Poker Chip of H. Matisse) - but hey, as that Meatloaf song says - one out of nineteen ain't bad! Or...something like that ;)<br /><br />This was a really fun collection to read during my road trip in New England - it was quite easy to fit in a story or two each evening. I did try and read some aloud to my boyfriend, but he did not remain awake until the end for ANY of them. However, I'm going to blame that on my soothing voice and not the stories themselves. <br /><br />My absolute FAVOURITE story was The Emissary. This was a sweet story about a sick young boy and his dog... that turns a little dark. I love it when stories turn dark unexpectedly. If you combine emotion with my horror, then I'm gonna be on board about 93% of the time.<br /><br />There's just so many memorable tales that I won't forget in a hurry! The Small Assassin, which is about a mother who is convinced that her newborn baby is out to kill her. The Scythe, a chilling tale about a man who comes into the possession of a powerful wheat field and an even more powerful scythe. The Lake (which was my other favourite story) is about a man revisiting his childhood home and recalling a friend who drowned in a lake during their childhood.<br /><br />Bradbury's writing is beautifully poetic and his stories incredibly inventive. I'm reading these stories quite a few years after they were written and they are just as impactful and unique. Bradbury is quickly becoming one of my favourite authors!<br /><br />Can't rave about this one enough! One of the best short story collections I've ever read - it's up there alongside King's Night Shift and Clive Barker's Books of Blood. All the stars!!! Or 5... if we're using the standard rating system.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7860627543343440006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_73575692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73575692" />
    <a title="Shawn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1697111-shawn"><img alt="Shawn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588366811p2/1697111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73575692?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shawn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shawn" href="/user/show/1697111-shawn">Shawn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345407856" href="/book/show/1091582.The_October_Country">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1697111-shawn?shelf=r-comp-h-s-20th-40-70-own">r-comp-h-s-20th-40-70-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73575692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13839868686313705667">One of the first books I ever read, and one of the reasons I still read. I found some of the other reviews dismaying (poor dialogue? silly concepts? antique writing style? - has the world and the people in it really changed that much? Have people lost their hearts? Perhaps, they've just never read "The Smile" by Bradbury, not included in this collection).<br/><br/>Granted, Bradbury's style does take some getting used to - the man is emotionally honest and as people everywhere become more emotionally guard</span>
  <span id="freeText13839868686313705667" style="display:none">One of the first books I ever read, and one of the reasons I still read. I found some of the other reviews dismaying (poor dialogue? silly concepts? antique writing style? - has the world and the people in it really changed that much? Have people lost their hearts? Perhaps, they've just never read "The Smile" by Bradbury, not included in this collection).<br /><br />Granted, Bradbury's style does take some getting used to - the man is emotionally honest and as people everywhere become more emotionally guarded, such honesty appears to be naivete. It isn't, but that's an argument for another day. And Bradbury occasionally enjoys being poetic or lyrical, so people marking time until they can rush through volume #17 of "Lilith McHotpants, Ace Ghoul Slayer"; "Part the Twelveteenth of the Saga of Kaaarfgaaasr", and "P is for Perfunctory" or whatever they spend the majority of their time "reading" may find such a style annoying. Because, you know, it's about evoking feelings and such - not pushing buttons.<br /><br />But for those with the eye for a well-told tale, and senses neither dulled by crap or so highly attuned by High Lit that they can't enjoy solid pulp, this should go down a treat.<br /><br />"The Dwarf" - still as sad and dark and painful as I remember it. You have to love the breezy way Bradbury can just roll a story along with a deft turn of phrase or description ("the sea was a burning sheet of tinsel and glass"). So sad but honest that the cruel person doesn't even see what's wrong, and suffers nothing, while the girl's attempt to be human and humane puts the chain of events into motion. And Mr. Bigelow wrote detective stories! Heh! I like the fact that there's no overt supernatural elements in this collection at first, the initial stories all turn on human psyches and neurosis until "The Skeleton".<br /><br />"The Next In Line" - notorious to me because it was so long I never finished it as a kid. Here, again, no overt supernatural elements, just a woman suddenly overwhelmed by the inescapable awareness of her own mortality, exacerbated by the horrors of unburied mummies of Mexican peasants (they can't afford the rent to remain buried anymore, in the most ultimate capitalist scam ever - something to keep in mind for our futures), the strangeness of culture shock and the unloving husband to whom she's already dead. This might be a tad overwrought/overwritten but the feverish pitch of her nervous breakdown really does drag you along and the scenes in the catacombs (counting to avoid the screaming dead - but you can't avoid them because here they are, and here, and here...and HERE!), the descriptions of the little Mexican town at night (the streetlight blowing in the wind), the desperate race to escape the town (but you can't escape death), the little details ("whirled and cavorted before the coffin-shaped mirror") and omens (a sugar skull with your name on it), are all aces! I especially liked the bit where she seeks escape and safety in reading writing from "her world" - news + pop magazines - but even those are consumed far too quickly. Also appreciated seeing the gestation for the idea of "Skeleton" in her comments on why skeletons do not bother her. Just great, solid writing.<br /><br />"The Watchful Poker Chip of H. Matisse" - Nice for a number of reasons, foremost in that it's a horror story but funny at the same time, the horror arising, again, from the psyche and how it responds to the attentions of groups (inspiration for "The Crowd" possibly?) and popular culture. Couldn't help thinking of that old George Carlin "Book Club" book title - "Self Mutilation As An Attention-Getter" as the story wrapped up, and that aspect is really where the dry, bitter horror lies (and why the story is oddly relevant today as well). Loved seeing <b>VIC &amp; SADE</b> get a name-check (the best OTR comedy show EVER!) but I guess that also makes me a hipster doofus like the rest of the Cellar Septet (great band name waiting to be snagged!). I know Bradbury's tone towards the avant gardeians, like the tone of most people towards the artistic fringe, is disdainful but personally I love characters like this, ironic posing or no. So Garvey's eventual transformation into a surrealist object is both sad and cool for me (the Surrealists knew that desire and death were intricately linked).<br /><br />"Skeleton" - this rocked my world as a little kid and only impresses me more as an adult. The concept is just wonderfully simple (man at war with his skeleton), then toss in the resonant symbolism (the organic, painful messiness of life - his outsides - pitted against the clean, orderly, solid, reliable support structure of his insides, which symbolize death. "Only the dead are eternally cool" - as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/110688.Hakim_Bey" title="Hakim Bey" rel="nofollow noopener">Hakim Bey</a> said), the little details (he's great at ceramics!), and even some unexpected argument from the other side (the heavy man's fat as a buffer against the battering of life, and a way to trap the skeleton in organic tar), topped off with a bizarre character that makes it a bone fide "weird tale" (*salty breadsticks*) and a memorable last line- you've got yourself a killer story.<br /><br />"The Jar" has been adapted a few times (I seem to remember a version on <b>The New Alfred Hitchcock Presents</b> TV show from the 80s in which the country bumpkin setting is switched with a modern art one) and it's a pretty solid, creepy story, although Bradbury's tendency to overwrite becomes a bit cartoonish when (at least, I feel) he's writing characters of a "type" that he's not directly familiar with. Still, the inchoate mass in the jar is strangely evocative (a blank screen onto which everyone projects their ideas) and it's a nice reversal of "The Watchful Poker Chip", in that one man desires to make himself the center of attention from people who don't really care about him.<br /><br />"The Lake" is very touching. I have a reading of this by Bradbury himself and it really is an effective, emotional piece about lost childhood love and the uncaring blankness of death.<br /><br />"The Emissary" still gives me chills. This was a favorite of mine as a child, and reading it as an adult, I wondered if perhaps it might have just seemed darker or more threatening to me back then, but no - the ending is ominously "not good". Bradbury really stretches his evocative language shtick here, conjuring autumn in a million ways while bolting the whole thing to a life-lived memory of being an invalid, and then wrapping it in a strange variation of "The Monkey's Paw"...except this time...the door gets to open....<br /><br />"Touched With Fire" was also adapted on <b>Alfred Hitchcock Presents</b> (albeit the original b&amp;w version). It is very well done and I like the fact that the terrible events partially arise from the main characters' desire to help people (as in "The Dwarf" and, inverted strangely, the presumption of "The Crowd" - which is proven wrong). The writing makes the harridan woman a bit cartoonish, but that's part of the point, to draw you into feeling what Foxe (or the other one, I forget) feels and this building of tone and mood (hot, irritable, loud, abrasive) is well-conjured. Cute.<br /><br />"The Small Assassin" is a genuine classic, postpartum depression twisted into a horror tale before there was even a term for it. The last line is a killer and the modern resonances it brings, of abortion and sociopatholgy, are especially powerful. What a great story!<br /><br />"The Crowd" is another one of those unabashed classics - a simple idea marvelously realized. It's amazing how effective it is. I don't even know if there's much more to say - I love the idea that the people encompass all character types and are immortal. I also love that there's never a specific explanation or explication from "the Crowd", so while the ending confirms the narrator's theory, we only ever really have his suppositions to go on. I wish more modern horror writers wrote stories this sharp.<br /><br />"Jack-In-The Box" is...okay. It almost seems like an idea more pregnant with possibilities than can reasonably be addressed in the short story form, although I do like idea that the kid thinks he's dead at the end, and that this year's special room was an elevator. For some reason, this story strikes me as a partial riff on Lovecraft's "The Outsider".<br /><br />"The Scythe" is another great one - solid, well-told, well-imagined, painful. Another great idea that doesn't need world-building or explication - just accept it - because Bradbury is such a good storyteller why would your ruin the story with more questions? It's like reading a young, creative person's first realization that death isn't fair and logical.<br /><br />"Uncle Einar" is, of course, not really a horror story, more of a weird tale in that fine old tradition. It's also one of his stories about "The Family" that eventually influenced Charles Addams. It's probably the slightest of those Family stories (Cecy's story, "The Traveler" is really dark!) and I've never read Bradbury's late-in-life reworking of this material into a novel-form, because I feel so close to "Homecoming" and The Family, et. al (having discovered them at exactly the right moment of my childhood). But this one is a wonderful bit of dark fantasy, touching and sweet.<br /><br />"The Wind" - a simple idea simply told, as long as it needs to be and no longer. I love how it locates the main narrative away from the important action, and then comments upon that very thing ("as we sit here, people are dying"), using the set-up for an effective punchline. Nice.<br /><br />"The Man Upstairs" really made an impact on me as a kid. With the imaginings of multicolored worlds seen through glass, the focus on innards of all types, the reinvention of the "vampire", and the implied gruesome ending dissection, this is a great creepy horror story. Again, even better for the lack of explanation.<br /><br />"There Was An Old Woman" is another charming weird tale, with an unexpected ending (usually, stories like this would be about acceptance of the inevitable). The cantankerous old biddy is strongly sketched and the humor is well-delivered.<br /><br />"The Cistern", slight but poetic, is more about evoking Ophelia-like images of drowned bodies and flowers deep underground than telling a full story.<br /><br />"The Wonderful Death of Dudley Stone" is also, I fear, a bit slight for my tastes. It's a cute idea and, as expected, well-told, but the central idea - eh, too romantic an envisioning of a "writer as character" for my tastes.<br /><br />I left "Homecoming" for last because its one of my favorite Bradbury stories ever, ur-text for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/52274.Charles_Addams" title="Charles Addams" rel="nofollow noopener">Charles Addams</a>'s famous Family, <b>The Munsters</b>, etc. and thus a component of the whole 1950's "Monster Kid" culture. It also still brings tears to my eyes. I hope he continued to walk the fine line the originals tread so assuredly (the "monsters" ARE monsters, as Cecy's interaction with the old woman and the mud-pits illustrates) in his later re-use of this material. I wonder if all the Tim Burton fans even know a story like this exists?<br /><br />Great stories from a great writer. What more could you want?</span>
  <a data-text-id="13839868686313705667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

  <span id="freeText17918533920942227546" style="display:none">Finally! Finally, I've read this classic collection of short stories by Ray Bradbury. I'm so glad that I did. <br /><br />What can I say about this that hasn't already been said? It's beautifully written. Some of the stories were so haunting, I know that they will stick with me for the rest of my life: Ones like "The Scythe" and "The Dwarf", "Jack in the Box" and "Homecoming." <br /><br />Bradbury's style is sparse; he packs a lot of meaning into very few words. His prose is so beautiful that, at times, the reader has to pause for a moment and savor it. Seriously, it's amazing.<br /><br />I highly recommend this collection of short stories written by one of the masters of the form!</span>

<div id="review_346176015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/346176015" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/346176015?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345407856" href="/book/show/1091582.The_October_Country">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer346176015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4436069929579692827">I am quite useless at reviewing an anthologies so please bear with me. How do you go about reviewing these things any way? Story by story? Sounds like a chore. I'll just muddle through as usual then!<br/><br/><i>The October Country</i> is a collection of Ray Bradbury's macabre stories, I hesitate to label them as "horror stories" because they are not particularly horrifying, but they are mostly odd and unsettling, almost "new weird" but disqualified on the "new" part! The iIllustrations by Joe Mugnaini (a few ar</span>
  <span id="freeText4436069929579692827" style="display:none">I am quite useless at reviewing an anthologies so please bear with me. How do you go about reviewing these things any way? Story by story? Sounds like a chore. I'll just muddle through as usual then!<br><br><i>The October Country</i> is a collection of Ray Bradbury's macabre stories, I hesitate to label them as "horror stories" because they are not particularly horrifying, but they are mostly odd and unsettling, almost "new weird" but disqualified on the "new" part! The iIllustrations by Joe Mugnaini (a few are shown below) add a surreal atmosphere to the stories. I will just run through them quickly:<br><br><b>"Dwarf"</b> - A little strange, ends before it should for me, no clear denouement, but a good little morality tale.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475457830i/20723133._SY540_.jpg" width="382" height="594" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>"The Next in Line"</b> - I like the gradation of depths for burial based on the wealth of the family. There is a very creepy, psychologically unsettling scene in the catacombs. I find the story goes on too long yet the ending is a little too abrupt (too "WTF" if you will). I do love the lyricism and poetic rhythm of his writing though.<br><b>"The Watchful Poker Chip of H. Matisse"</b> - A humorous observation about the pretentious avant garde. Nice one!<br><b>"Skeleton"</b> - That is gross man! LOL! A darkly humorous story. OK, this one is <i>a bit</i> horrifying. I love it!<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475457830i/20723134._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>"The Jar"</b> - I like how the entire story revolves around the mysterious content of the jar, the twist at the end is cool.<br><b>"The Lake"</b> - The first overtly supernatural tale in this book, a sentimental and sad little ghost story.<br><b>"The Emissary"</b> - Unexpected twist in this one, to say any more would spoil it. Great story!<br><b>"Touched With Fire"</b> - The story of a couple of retired insurance salesmen and a perpetually angry woman who is a sort of Angry Bird in human form.<br><b>"The Small Assassin"</b> - My favorite story in this book, give me the collywobbles! I'm not saying anything about the plot!<br><b>"The Crowd"</b> - The crowd that materialize in accident scenes are indeed reprehensible. Good story.<br><b>"Jack-in-the-Box"</b> - A weird scifi-ish story. Have you seen M. Night Shyamalan 2004 movie <i>The Village</i>? This story is a bit like that (well, no, that movie is a bit like this, which predates it).<br><b>"The Scythe"</b> - Don't fear the Reaper, or don't fear the reaping. This story left a singularly eerie image in my mind after reading.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475457830i/20723135._SY540_.jpg" width="382" height="623" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>"Uncle Einar"</b> - A pleasant, lighthearted story. Not creepy at all.<br><b>"The Wind"</b> - Supernatural, creepy and sad.<br><b>"The Man Upstairs</b>" - an excellent vampire tale with triangles, chains, and pyramids - genius!<br><b>"There Was an Old Woman"</b> - Annoying bloody woman, good for a giggle.<br><b>"The Cistern"</b> - A weird watery tale which is almost a ghost story.<br><b>"Homecoming"</b> - "They're creepy and they're kooky, Mysterious and spooky" - actually may be more like "The Munster".<br><b>"The Wonderful Death of Dudley Stone"</b> - Strange mainstream-ish story. It is actually quite wonderful, a great way to end the book.<br><br><b>TL;DR:</b> My favorite stories are "Skeleton", "The Small Assassin" and "The Wonderful Death of Dudley Stone".<br><br>Probably not Bradbury's best collection but Bradbury is always well worth reading. The best Ray Bradbury anthology is - IMO - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24830.The_Illustrated_Man" title="The Illustrated Man by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">The Illustrated Man</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="The Martian Chronicles by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">The Martian Chronicles</a> is great but it is more of a fix-up novel.<br><br>As I write it is almost Halloween, the best time to read this collection!<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1443700868i/16397956._SY540_.jpg" width="382" height="600" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> </span>
  <a data-text-id="4436069929579692827" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_564423467" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/564423467" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/564423467?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345407856" href="/book/show/1091582.The_October_Country">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=classic-horror">classic-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=october-country">october-country</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer564423467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7329624287479605700"><b>
  <i>October Country...that country where it is always turning late in the year. That country where the hills are fog and the rivers are mist; where noons go quickly, dusks and twilights linger, and midnights stay. That country composed in the main of cellars, sub-cellars, coal bins, closets, attics, and pantries faced away from the sun. That country whose people are autumn people, thinking only autumn thoughts. Whose people passing at night on the empty walks sound like rain...</i>
</b><br/><br/>Fall is probably m</span>
  <span id="freeText7329624287479605700" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>October Country...that country where it is always turning late in the year. That country where the hills are fog and the rivers are mist; where noons go quickly, dusks and twilights linger, and midnights stay. That country composed in the main of cellars, sub-cellars, coal bins, closets, attics, and pantries faced away from the sun. That country whose people are autumn people, thinking only autumn thoughts. Whose people passing at night on the empty walks sound like rain...</i>
</b><br /><br />Fall is probably my favorite of all seasons, and every year I walk on the streets, through avenues and parks. There is a smell of burning leaves hanging around lazily, and the skies are still bright, sharp and clear, but the sun is less warm. You can feel the wind getting colder and taste the air, now sharper and fresher. Nights are chilling, with big yellow moons. Leaves change their colors and are now a mixture of yellow, green, red and orange. They start dropping from the trees one by one at first, but steadily gusts of wind grasp them by the handfuls and leave bare branches behind. Although the process is inherently sad in nature as it forecasts the upcoming winter, with its ice and snow, there is an element of beauty in fall leaves on the ground, especially in the afternoon sunlight. It casts a special shine which is not there in other seasons, and yellowing leaves make the streets look as if they were paved with gold.<br /><br />My copy of <i>The October Country</i> has a new introduction by Ray Bradbury, written in 1999, where he claims to remembering being born and the development of his passion for stories and storytelling. He wrote his first story in the seventh grade, and since the age of twelve knew that was the way to ensure proper immortality - being remembered after our limited time on earth runs out. Bradbury saw the process of writing as a match between life and death, each completed story a victory. Days when he didn't write were threatening him extinction, and this is why he wrote every day since he turned twelve, evading death. He died last year, at the age of 91, having published his last novel - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9620.Farewell_Summer" title="Farewell Summer by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">Farewell Summer</a> - six years before, along with hundreds of short stories. Death has finally caught with Ray, but not before he had his say - he went out on his own terms, and achieved the exact type of immortality that he hoped for.<br /><br /><i>The October Country</i> contains nineteen very different stories, most of which were previously published in Bradbury's debut colection, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1794059.Dark_Carnival" title="Dark Carnival by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">Dark Carnival</a>. These are some of Bradbury's earliest stories, published before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="The Martian Chronicles by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">The Martian Chronicles</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17470674.Fahrenheit_451" title="Fahrenheit 451 by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">Fahrenheit 451</a>. There is a special pleasure in reading these stories - allowing Ray Bradbury's gentle storytelling lull us in and expose us to his imagination. I can see young Kings and Mathesons of this world reading his fiction deep into the night, possibly even with a flashlight under the bedcovers, amazed that a grownup could think such things onto paper. Bradbury was a man who could both turn a phrase and had a great, big heart - his warmth emanates from his writing, which is far removed from the vulgarity of contemporary world. Bradbury was often accused of being too sentimental and too emotional, but I don't think this is completely correct - while it's true that his stories offer a bucolic vision of the American heartland and the nostalgia of small-town life, but didn't shy away from showing the nastiness running behind the curtain of these idealistic visions.<br /><br />This is perhaps best illustrated in the opening story, <i>The Dwarf</i>, which is an achingly sad story of loneliness and cruelty. What makes it sad is that not only the cruelty is pointless - as it always is - but that it's inflicted on someone who is in no capability to defend himself, and by someone who makes an extra effort to make sure that it hits where it hurts the most. There's no need for supernatural elements here - ordinary life is enough, as events like these happen every day, everywhere. <br /><br /><i>The Lake</i> is another touching and sad story, but in another way - it's about a man who revisits his childhood home and is flooded with memories of a lost friend. It's almost a ghost story, but not quite - the ghosts are the memories which flood the main character to the point where he almost re-enters the past, and feels disconnected from - and disappointed with - the present reality.<br /><br />There are great stories with a touch of horror here, too. <i>The Skeleton</i> and The Small Assassin both have great, imaginative premises and work very well. The first one features a man convinced that his skeleton is out to kill him - and tries to fight it; it concludes with a great, memorable last line, true to style of classic short fiction. <i>The Small Assassin</i> is Bradbury's experiment with psychological horror - a woman becomes convinced that her newborn baby is conspiring to kill her. While this might seem to be just an example of postnatal stress and depression, the arguments she uses sound at least a bit true: is there anything in the world more selfish than a baby, with its unending demand for constant care and attention? Do some mothers (and fathers) do not have the feeling that sometimes their baby is acting the way it does just to spite and annoy them? This is a great horror story without vampires or boogeymen, but with cribs, nappies and milk bottles. The last line, again, is a killer - literally!<br /><br />There are more horrific stories here: <i>The Next in Line</i> features a couple exploring a cemetery in a small Mexican town which holds a truly capitalistic policy towards the buried and their families; <i>The Crowd</i> is another great story which truly emphasizes Bradbury's great strength: exploring simple ideas and writing classic tales based on them. Its narrator finds it odd that crowds of spectators always gather around the scene of an accident, and finds people who would stare at someone's tragedy reprehensible: the truth is stranger than any of us could suspect. Same goes for <i>The wind</i>, where a man is obsessed with wind - he's an adventurer who thinks that wind has always been out there to get him, and even though he always managed to escape he's convinced that this time his luck has finally ran out. <i>There was an Old Woman</i> is different, in the way that it takes a scary situation and makes it amusing: it's concerned with an old, cantankerous woman who just refuses to die. It's good and full of fun humor.<br /><br />There's a great mixture of Bradbury's recollection of his youth mixed with his interest in weirdness in <i>Uncle Einar</i> and Homecoming, both of which share characters. <i>Uncle Einar</i> was inspired by Bradbury's favorite uncle, and you can see his love in this weird tale of a man with wings who longs to return to the skies but has to live among people who don't have them. The resolution is heartwarming and memorable. <i>Homecoming</i> is the exact reverse of <i>Uncle Einar</i> - Timothy, its young narrator, is a mortal child living among supernatural beings. Left on their doorstep as an infant, he longs to be like them but at the same time understands that this will never be possible. Unlike <i>Uncle Einar</i>, <i>Homecoming</i> is a sad story of a boy who wishes to belong but will always be an outsider, even with the complete support of his adoptive family.<br /><br />My favorite story from the entire collection is probably <i>The Scythe</i>, which is a great, imaginative story, in the nature of the folk tales that I read as a young boy. Set in what looks like the Depression, a poor family notices an empty house at the end of a road, and upon entering discovers the body of a previous inhabitant and his will - bequeathing both the house and farm behind it to the person reading it. The family is overjoyed at first, and after a few days of rest the father cannot sit still anymore and goes to cut wheat in the field - only to discover strange things about it, and the way it grows. It's a great story in the classic tradition of moral tales - that there will always be a price to pay for what seems to be too good to be true, and that we should be wary of things which seem to be just incredible luck.<br /><br /><i>The Wonderful Death of Dudley Stone</i> is a great coda - Dudley Stone is an extraordinarily successful writer who has quit the profession at the age of 30, without explanation, leaving people without any certainty if he was even alive or dead. 25 years later a group of his most devoted fans cannot bear not knowing, and one of them decides to go to Stone's hometown and find information about him. It's a great story to end the collection, a meditation on the choices we make in life and the things we set as priorities, with a beautiful last line.<br /><br />I could very well discuss all the stories, but I believe that the reader deserves the pleasure to discover the October Country on their own, especially with illustrations by Joe Mugnaini. So come along and open the book to visit that country, the country dusks and twilights linger, and midnights stay, whose people are autumn people thinking autumn thoughts, and who on the empty walks at night sound like rain...<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7329624287479605700" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2642073823?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2642073823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9904878365403547984">Watch "The Twilight Show" (even any other "derivative" of that show for that matter, i.e. anything from Perversions of Science to Black Mirror to Tales from the Crypt) and those goosebumps that develop ever so sinister-like? Every story in this collection earns that type of unforgettable excitement: the pure thrill of being scared. And by no other than this maestro. Hard to believe that the master was with me, personally, all along. I still remember coming across "Something Wicked this Way Comes</span>
  <span id="freeText9904878365403547984" style="display:none">Watch "The Twilight Show" (even any other "derivative" of that show for that matter, i.e. anything from Perversions of Science to Black Mirror to Tales from the Crypt) and those goosebumps that develop ever so sinister-like? Every story in this collection earns that type of unforgettable excitement: the pure thrill of being scared. And by no other than this maestro. Hard to believe that the master was with me, personally, all along. I still remember coming across "Something Wicked this Way Comes" in middle school and thinking how awful that true terror is available to all people equally: you are not immune because you of your sex, race, and age. It is a common thread that unites us; whether it be bodily terror, or psychological torment, or straight-up monster mash. Being scared.<br /><br />My favorite short story (all of them are as perfect as can be): Skeleton.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1090610409" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1090610409" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1090610409?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345407856" href="/book/show/1091582.The_October_Country">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1090610409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10501289068483693169"><br/><i> ... that country were it is always turning late in the year. That country where the hills are fog and the rivers are mist; where noons go quickly, dusks and twilight linger, and midnights stay. The country composed in the main of cellars, sub-cellars, coal-bins, closets, attics, and pantries faced away from the sun. That country whose people are autumn people, thinking only autumn thoughts. Whose people passing at night on the empty walks sound like rain ... </i><br/><br/>The introduction suggests this one's</span>
  <span id="freeText10501289068483693169" style="display:none"><br /><i> ... that country were it is always turning late in the year. That country where the hills are fog and the rivers are mist; where noons go quickly, dusks and twilight linger, and midnights stay. The country composed in the main of cellars, sub-cellars, coal-bins, closets, attics, and pantries faced away from the sun. That country whose people are autumn people, thinking only autumn thoughts. Whose people passing at night on the empty walks sound like rain ... </i><br /><br />The introduction suggests this one's a good choice for a Halloween read, and indeed the stories selected for the present collection may not all happen in October, but they do share a melancholic mood that often morphs into full blown fright. I am thinking of adding as a subtitle <b> High Anxiety </b> for the whole set and then play a game of 'Name That Fear' for each episode:<br /><br /><b> The Dwarf </b> is a great opening gambit, probably one of the earliest shorts written by the author, illustrating his fascination with carnivals and with the grotesque. A short man visits every night the hall of mirrors in the amusement park, always heading straight for the one distorting silverbacked glass that makes him appear as tall as regular people. The ticket vendor makes fun of him with the callous indifference of bullies everywhere. The anxiety here would be the loneliness of the person rejected from society for being different in appearance. I liked how the dwarf is presented as a succesful pulp writer, how inside his head he's just as good, if not better, than his tormentors:<br /><br /><i> This little guy's got a soul as big as all outdoors; he's got everything in his head! </i><br /><br /><b> The Next In Line </b> highlights the fear of the cemeteries, of the dark, damp and smelly place undergound where the dead are buried. A young couple on a tourist visit to Mexcio comes across a town where the air is so dry that bodies do not rot in the ground and are instead mummified. Because a lot of the local people are too poor to pay for the burial place, these mummies are exhumated and stored in a long underground chamber and then shown to the tourists for a small fee. I have saved a quote from this story, where the young man chides his wife for being superstitious, but I have a hunch that the author sides with her on the issue, as sometimes the fear is too strong for the rational brain:<br /><br /><i> The minute you get a religion you stop thinking. Believe in one thing too much and you have no room for new ideas. </i><br /><br /><b> The Watchful Poker Chip of H. Matisse </b> doesn't seem too scary at first glance - it is the story of a man so dull and uninteresting that he becomes an attraction for a crowd of fashionable artists. Enjoying their attention, the man now tries to remain in the spotlight by artificial means, like wearing an unusual eye-piece painted by the famous French Impressionist. My vote would be again for fear of loneliness, of rejection by social conformists.<br /><br /><b> Skeleton </b> is fear of illness, of your own body. It is the tale of a hypochondriac who goes to a dubious doctor and becomes aware that he has been walking around all his life carrying a skeleton inside him. Delightfully macabre.<br /><br /><b> The Jar </b> is a second story that starts with a carnival and its gallery of freaks. It is a variant of the fear of the unknown, and a story about the way our imagination can create monsters out of unexplained physical or biological objects. Here, a farmer becomes fascinated with one of those pale and wobbly things floating in chloroform in big transparent bottles. Ignorance and superstition also play a role, as the man's neighbors from a poor swamp village gather around the jar to speculate on the thing's origins.<br /><br /><b> The Lake </b> is about fear of death by drowning, but more than this it is about the passing of childhood, about lost friends and the power of love to keep the memories alive. One of the most lyrical and sad shorts in the whole collection.<br /><br /><b> The Emissary </b> is another variant of the fear of death. In this short story, a boy is immobilized in bed by illness, and his trusty dog is his only contact with the outside world, by bringing in the twigs and burrs and the smells of the places it visits. But what happens when this dog starts visiting a cemetery too often?<br /><br /><b> Touched With Fire </b> is more difficult to pin down. Two mysterious strangers try to prevent a woman from getting murdered. Are they prescient or simply better observers of human nature than usual? I would class the story as fear of predestination, of the loss of free will, but a more accurate message may be that we cannot force people to act against their nature.<br /><br /><b> The Small Assassin </b> is a creepy example of a woman with post-natal depression, who believes her child tried to kill her during a difficult birth, and is continuing to attack her after they return home from hospital. It's a clever piece, but not one of my favorites: it fels contrived, even if I accept the supernatural elements.<br /><br /><b> The Crowd </b> is an illustration of a fear I was spared from until recently. I got my driver's license only a couple of years ago, and my first car only months ago. Since then, I have started to consider more seriously what would happen if I got into a road accident. Bradbury doesn't help me much as he tries to prove that there is a reason you always see a crowd of thrill seekers around the sites of such crashes, and that these people are not there to help you. <br /><br /><b> Jack-In-The-Box </b> is about a boy who is afraid the whole world outside his house is gone. He is locked in with his mother and a mysterious teacher inside a huge house with many locked doors and secret passages. He would like to escape, yet is afraid of what would happen if he breaks out of his safe daily routine. <br /><br /><b> The Scythe </b> is a good candidate for a 'Best of ...' anthology of Bradbury short stories. It's major anxiety is the fear of a father that he cannot provide sustenance and safety for his family during the Great Depression, most of all that he cannot protect them from the death that must come to us all, sooner or later. Great writing!<br /><br /><b> Uncle Einar </b> is one of the few happy interludes in an otherwise sad and scary collection. It is a sketch from a bigger story the author was developing about a family of monsters ( The Elliots are similar in many aspects to the more famous Adams Family). Einar is a sort of human bat, six feet tall with big leathery green wings. He flies mostly at night to avoid being spotted by regular people, but after a drunken party, he crashes into a power line and loses his sonar-like abilities. But, like somebody sung about in the Alps, whenever a door closes, a window opens, and by losing his fly-in-the-dark talents, Einar gains the love of a woman and settles down for married life and for playing with his children. My favorite quote here is about how we may be homely in our outside appearance, but there is a world of wonder and imagination inside each of us:<br /><br /><i> We're in our cocoons, all of us. See how ugly I am? But one day I'll break out, spread wings as fine and handsome as you. </i><br /><br /><b> The Wind </b> is another candidate for 'best of' anthologies, very short but also very effective in the idea that destructive winds somehow achieve intelligence by absorbing the minds of their victims. The fear category in my game could be the anxiety over the unleashed forces of nature.<br /><br /><b> The Man Upstairs </b> is a variant of the fear of the stranger, about supernatural predators living amonst us, disguised as ordinary people. What makes the story special is the young age of the narrator, a young boy whose curiosity and inventivity solves the mystery. Word of the day from his grandfather:<br /><br /><i> Fear nothing, ever in your life. </i><br /><br /><b> There Was an Old Woman </b> is another of the rare stories with a touch of humour, with a colourful elderly lady as a heroine who refuses to accept the inevitability of Death, and is ready to fight to the last breath and beyond for the right to stay in this world as much as it pleases her.<br /><br /><b> The Cistern </b> is a sort of twisted romance spiced by the fear of drowning. A woman gazes out a window at a rainy city landscape and imagines the water draining into subterranean tunnels, filing them up a carrying along the bodies of strangers.<br /><br /><b> Homecoming </b> is almost worth the price of admission all on its own. It marks a return to the follies of the Elliott family, as they gather from all over the world to celebrate Halloween together. Uncle Einar also returns to lift up the spirits of a boy without supernatural talents, adopted into the family and slightly envious of his 'monster' relatives. The story has a lot of potential, and I understand Bradbury added more material until it became a novel. Anxiety in this case is the result of feeling estranged from your peers.<br /><br /><b> The Wonderful Death of Dudley Stone </b> ends the book not with another scare but with an affirmation of life, an invitation to get our noses out of books and run around in the sun. Dudley Moore was a very promising young writer who decided decades ago to renounce his literary career and fade into oblivion. In the present time, he invites a reporter to tell him all about that past decision and the reason for his seclusion:<br /><br /><i> I had been writing about living. Now I wanted to live. Do things instead of tell about things. [...] We've lived every way there is to live, with our eyes and noses and mouths, with our ears and hands. </i><br /><br />Taken as a whole I found the collection uneven in quality. Some stories feel unpolished, some just simple sketches, but then quite a few are truly memorable, and even the lesser ones display the magical way Bradbury has of creating a mood, an emotion, an intriguing new way to look at the ordinary things around us and see either their beauty or their mystery. Beside the theme of autumn and anxiety, the stories collected here share an interest in childhood, in the ties of family and in the need for friendship and sharing. I am glad I still have so many of his stories and novels still to read. I am sure they will be as enjoyable and well written as this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10501289068483693169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2755045272" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2755045272" />
    <a title="Jamie Stewart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15954768-jamie-stewart"><img alt="Jamie Stewart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551013124p2/15954768.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2755045272?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie Stewart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie Stewart" href="/user/show/15954768-jamie-stewart">Jamie Stewart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2755045272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15963935102186631264">The October Country: a book of strange, sometimes creepy, sometimes heart warming tales, written in Ray Bradbury’s flawless excitable style. You can tell from these pages that the man loved to write, in someways his words are like those of a child’s discovering a new love for the first time, it’s an enthusiasm that captivated me. <br/><br/>My favourite stories within this book are: <br/><br/>The Dwarf<br/>The Jar <br/>The Lake<br/>The Emissary<br/>The Little Assassin<br/>Uncle Einar<br/><br/>I noticed when reading them that it wasn’t until each stor</span>
  <span id="freeText15963935102186631264" style="display:none">The October Country: a book of strange, sometimes creepy, sometimes heart warming tales, written in Ray Bradbury’s flawless excitable style. You can tell from these pages that the man loved to write, in someways his words are like those of a child’s discovering a new love for the first time, it’s an enthusiasm that captivated me. <br /><br />My favourite stories within this book are: <br /><br />The Dwarf<br />The Jar <br />The Lake<br />The Emissary<br />The Little Assassin<br />Uncle Einar<br /><br />I noticed when reading them that it wasn’t until each story was finished that I could fully contemplate what I had just read. Take The Emissary for example it begins as this brilliant, heart warming story that sinks into terror only in the last three paragraphs. I also noticed that in a lot of these stories it’s left for the readers imagination in such stories as The Next In Line, Jack In The Box and The Crowd. The Little Assassin is terrifying throughout. The Lake is one of the most touching pieces of fiction I’ve never read.<br /><br />Don’t look to be scared is my only recommendation with these stories, come at this book with no expectations and it will hold a bounty of surprises for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15963935102186631264" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570515900?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2018-shelf">2018-shelf</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570515900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10980653890256348158">This might be a re-read... but for the most part, I had forgotten much of what I read here from way back in the day.<br/><br/>No matter.<br/><br/>It's odd. I've changed as a reader. These slow and gently transformative stories are... prosaic. They don't grab me as much as they might have, years ago. Indeed, I dropped a star for that reason. But I still found enough to love in them that I didn't just despair from boredom.<br/><br/>For one, I'm familiar enough with so many movies and tv shows and even music to exclaim... "Hey!</span>
  <span id="freeText10980653890256348158" style="display:none">This might be a re-read... but for the most part, I had forgotten much of what I read here from way back in the day.<br /><br />No matter.<br /><br />It's odd. I've changed as a reader. These slow and gently transformative stories are... prosaic. They don't grab me as much as they might have, years ago. Indeed, I dropped a star for that reason. But I still found enough to love in them that I didn't just despair from boredom.<br /><br />For one, I'm familiar enough with so many movies and tv shows and even music to exclaim... "Hey! They took that from Bradbury!" or "Hey! Someone really ran with a Bradbury idea and made it deadly!" or "This is superior to Bradbury!"<br /><br />Ahem. Bradbury has great ideas! Bradbury has wonderful prose! Yes. But he's also mild. I love writers that take ideas and do something extraordinary, and back when these were written, that was probably the case.<br /><br />Something to consider: His story "Touched with Fire" has a great, perhaps apocryphal, line about more murders occurring at 92 degrees F than any other temperature. It was used in the 70's B movie It Came From Outer Space. And then it was used in a great song by Siouxsie and the Banshees. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=YIqyVsZGxi0">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=YIqyV...</a><br /><br />I was all, like... I love that song! Another fun fact, Siouxsie's punk music also does full tributes to Stephen King and Shakespeare. Much love. :)<br /><br />Oh, I hated the story "The Wonderful Death of Dudley Stone" :)<br /><br />Everything else was fine, if not super-grabbing. :)</span>
